        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b05687"><meta property="og:title" content="Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/acsami.7b05687.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be a promising commercial alternative to lithium-ion batteries for grid electricity storage due to their potential low cost in the near future. Up to the present, the anode material still remains a great challenge for the application of SIBs, especially at room temperature. Graphite has an obvious limitation to store larger radius sodium ions (Na+) in comparison with lithium ions (Li+), while the hard carbon with large interlayer distance can demonstrate a relatively high storage capability and durable cycle life. However, the disadvantages of low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) mainly caused by large surface area and high cost synthetic approach hinder its practical applications. Herein, a new coupled carbonization strategy is presented to prepare a cost-effective hard carbon material by pyrolyzing and carbonizing the mixture of abundant sucrose and phenolic resin. Benefiting from the specialized pyrolysis reaction process and optimized conditions as studied in detail, the hard carbon has an extremely low surface area of 1.54 m2 g–1 and high initial Coulombic efficiency of 87%, which have been rarely reported before and enhance the utilization efficiency of Na+ consumption within the cathode in the future. More importantly, the hard carbon, with a high interlayer distance 3.95 Å, can deliver a higher capacity of 319 mAh g–1 and maintain a finer capacity retention of 90% over 150 cycles. Besides, a full cell with the configuration of as-prepared hard carbon anode versus an air-stable O3–Na0.9[Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.48]O2 cathode is further presented, and it has a high ICE of 80% and energy density of 256 Wh kganode–1 (vs hard carbon) with reliable cycle performance. The results demonstrate that our synthetic strategy is feasible and extendable, while the tunable carbon-based materials should have wider applications in addition to the attractive properties in Na-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/acsami.7b05687.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '46911837-aa30-456b-bea0-9ec28aacad28'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

Img" class="loading-img"></div></div><div class="figure-viewer_links"><a href="#" title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-powerpoint-o"></i><span>Download to MS-PowerPoint</span></a><span class="cit-this-container"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.7b05687" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i></span><span class="cit-fg-year"> 2017</span><span class="cit-fg-volume">, 9</span><span class="cit-fg-issue">, 28</span><span class="cit-fg-pageRange">, 23766-23774</span></span></span></div></div></div></div></div><input type="hidden" name="aamick" id="copyRights" value="/pb-assets/json/journal-info-1738870756703.json"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone5" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone5"><!-- Article Refresh Event Tracking -->
<script nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Coupled Carbonization Strategy toward Advanced Hard Carbon for High-Energy Sodium-Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b05687", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D23766%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHuimin%2BZhang%252C%2BHai%2BMing%252C%2BWenfeng%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D28%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b05687%26title%3DCoupled%2BCarbonization%2BStrategy%2Btoward%2BAdvanced%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bfor%2BHigh-Energy%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D23774%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2017" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be a promising commercial alternative to lithium-ion batteries for grid electricity storage due to their potential low cost in the near future. Up to the present, the anode material still remains a great challenge for the application of SIBs, especially at room temperature. Graphite has an obvious limitation to store larger radius sodium ions (Na<sup>+</sup>) in comparison with lithium ions (Li<sup>+</sup>), while the hard carbon with large interlayer distance can demonstrate a relatively high storage capability and durable cycle life. However, the disadvantages of low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) mainly caused by large surface area and high cost synthetic approach hinder its practical applications. Herein, a new coupled carbonization strategy is presented to prepare a cost-effective hard carbon material by pyrolyzing and carbonizing the mixture of abundant sucrose and phenolic resin. Benefiting from the specialized pyrolysis reaction process and optimized conditions as studied in detail, the hard carbon has an extremely low surface area of 1.54 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and high initial Coulombic efficiency of 87%, which have been rarely reported before and enhance the utilization efficiency of Na<sup>+</sup> consumption within the cathode in the future. More importantly, the hard carbon, with a high interlayer distance 3.95 Å, can deliver a higher capacity of 319 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and maintain a finer capacity retention of 90% over 150 cycles. Besides, a full cell with the configuration of as-prepared hard carbon anode versus an air-stable O3–Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode is further presented, and it has a high ICE of 80% and energy density of 256 Wh kg<sub>anode</sub><sup>–1</sup> (vs hard carbon) with reliable cycle performance. The results demonstrate that our synthetic strategy is feasible and extendable, while the tunable carbon-based materials should have wider applications in addition to the attractive properties in Na-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D23766%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHuimin%2BZhang%252C%2BHai%2BMing%252C%2BWenfeng%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D28%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b05687%26title%3DCoupled%2BCarbonization%2BStrategy%2Btoward%2BAdvanced%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bfor%2BHigh-Energy%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D23774%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon">carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbonization&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbonization">carbonization</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_battery">battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode">cathode</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries have overwhelmingly dominated the energy market, from hand-held electronic devices, grid storage, to the thriving electric vehicles, since its commercialization by Sony Company in the 1991s. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> However, the fast increase in demand has encountered the problem of limited and uneven distribution of lithium sources on Earth, where the Li<sup>+</sup> quantitatively behaves as the medium of energy transportation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3-5)</a> Alternatively, the rich reserves of the sodium element and related physiochemical properties of sodium- and lithium-based compounds have become a strong drive to stimulate more and more researchers to pay attention to the development of Na-ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6, 7)</a> Unfortunately, the commercialized graphite anode in Li-ion battery has a serious limitation to store the larger radius Na<sup>+</sup> and is difficult to use directly anymore, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> even with the excellent advancements that have been made in electrolyte and materials design. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9, 10)</a> Thus, development of a commercially available anode is urgently needed to match the cathode for applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">Although numerous candidates, including the carbon-based materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 11 to 14">(11-14)</a> metal (oxide), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 15 to 19">(15-19)</a> sulfide, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20, 21)</a> phosphorus, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> binary compounds, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> and carboxylates, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> are being widely investigated, the hard carbon anode is still the most promising and closest to the practical applications in terms of safety issues and energy density. The strict requirements of reversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability, limited volume expansion, and appropriate low voltage (e.g., ∼0.3–0.4 V) are even more important than those in commercial Li-ion batteries due to the higher activities of sodium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25, 26)</a> However, the hard carbon usually suffers several fatal weaknesses, while most mainly result from the structural characteristics determined by the synthetic approaches. For example, the fast capacity fading and in particular the low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) less than 75% are the biggest challenges, which are mainly caused by the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer on the large surface area and the irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the amorphous structure. The large irreversible capacity would consume one part of Na<sup>+</sup> from the cathode and cause them to be invalid, thereby leading to a low CE, a poor energy capacity, and inferior cycle ability of full battery. Therefore, seeking an efficient strategy to control a low surface area of carbon, maintaining a high Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability, is significant to improving its ICE and then allowing the practical applications of Na-ion batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we present a new coupled carbonization strategy to synthesize hard carbon materials, in which the mixture of sucrose and phenolic resin was directly pyrolyzed and carbonized. Although the carbohydrates such as the glucose and sucrose have been reported to synthesize the carbon-based materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27-29)</a> ICE from 54% to the highest 83%, capacity around 300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and specific surface area higher than 5.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> still have plenty of room to improve, and even graphene oxide additive was used to suppress the foaming of sucrose. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Besides, a low carbon yield less than 20%, caused by the foaming process and thermal evaporation of pyrolysis intermediates, is impracticable considering its sustainability and cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> In this study, we find that the phenol formaldehyde resins (PFs) can significantly change the carbonization process of sucrose. The sucrose–PF-derived hard carbon has a high yield of 35% and extremely low surface area of 1.54 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>; meanwhile, it can demonstrate a high ICE of 87% and a high capacity of 319 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The performances of as-prepared carbon materials are much better than the products from the sucrose or PF individually and also better than those reported as hard carbon derived from the PF-based organic precursors (e.g., capacity, 250–311 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, ICE, less than 60%) in Na-ion battery, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32, 33)</a> which may be attributed to the lower surface area and larger interlayer distance of as-prepared carbon materials. The specialized carbonization reaction process with the combined advantages of different carbon sources are systematically investigated, and the properties of hard carbon in full battery are further explored in addition to the sodium battery (vs sodium metal).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Hard carbon materials (HCMs) were prepared by pyrolysis of the mixture of sucrose (Analytical grade, Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.) and phenol formaldehyde resin (PF, Zhang Jiagang Oshika Industrial, Co., Ltd.). Typically, the precursors of sucrose and PF with the mass ratio of 2/1 were dissolved in aqueous solution and stirred for 30 min at 60 °C in beaker. Then the slurry was dried at 120 °C for 24 h (it was also used for FTIR and TGA analysis) and further carbonized at 200 °C for 1 h in a tube furnace under argon flow. The selected carbonization temperatures are 1000, 1200, 1400, and 1600 °C, where the obtained carbon materials are denoted as HCM-1000, HCM-1200, HCM-1400, and HCM-1600, respectively. The detailed process is described in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Scheme S1</a>, and the mass ratio of sucrose/PF is further tuned from 1:0 to 0:1 for different types of carbon materials.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The structural information was characterized by using an X-ray diffractometer (XRD) (XRD, Bruker/D8 advance) and Raman spectra (LabRAM HR800, JY Horiba). The morphologies were observed with a scanning electron microscope (SEM, Hitachi BCPCAS-4800, and Tokyo, Japan). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns were recorded on an FEI-Tecnai G2 F30 transmission electron microscope. The thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) was obtained using a NETZSCH STA 409 PC Luxx simultaneous thermal analyzer (Germany) from 30 to 1000 °C (maintaining for 1 h at 200 °C) at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under nitrogen atmosphere. The nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm was measured using a Micromeritics ASAP 2020 instrument, and the total specific surface area was calculated based on the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) model.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical electrode was prepared as below. Typically, the active material (i.e., hard carbon materials) and sodium alginate binder with the weight ratio of 9.5/0.5 were mixed in aqueous solution to form a uniform slurry and then casted on Cu foil. The electrode was dried at 100 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h and punched into a circular electrode before use. The loading mass of the hard carbon materials is controlled at around 2.0–3.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>, corresponding to the cathode loading of 6.5–10.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The sodium battery has a configuration of electrode | glass fiber separator | sodium foil, in which the electrolyte of 0.8 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1/1 in volume) was used. The battery was assembled in an argon-filled glovebox, and the electrochemical tests were carried out using the CR2032-type coin cell. The (dis-)charge curves were recorded at 30 °C using a Land BT2000 battery test system (Wuhan, China) in the voltage range of 0–2.0 V. For the Na-ion full battery, the O3–Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> and HCM-1400 were utilized as cathode and anode, respectively, where the Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> electrode was synthesized and prepared accordingly. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The weight ratio of anode/cathode was 1/3.4, and the voltage window of battery was controlled at 1.0–4.06 V based on the knowledge of full battery construction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35, 36)</a></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Features of As-Prepared Hard Carbon</h3><div class="NLM_p">One significant feature of as-prepared hard carbon materials (HCMs) is the extremely low specific surface area around 1.54 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a), which is almost the lowest value reported as far as we know. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref33 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref33 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 30, 33 and 37 to 43">(30, 33, 37-43)</a> With varying the carbonization temperature and the mass ratio of sucrose/PF, the surface area of carbon materials is tunable and always the lowest compared to those carbonized from the individual precursor of PF (29.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and sucrose (61.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, in which the black, blue, and red colors present the specific surface area, ICE, and interlayer distance of the HCM, respectively). It demonstrates that the coupled carbonization strategy is efficient to reduce the porosity and surface area. The optimal temperature for desired low surface area is around 1400 °C; however, there is no clear difference in morphologies within the temperature range of 1000–1600 °C, and they are all bulk particles with the size of 1–20 μm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), which are mainly determined by the sol–gel process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Specific surface area of as-prepared hard carbon materials compared to those in previous literature. Relationships of specific surface area (black color), initial Coulombic efficiency (blue color), and <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> space of hard carbon materials (red color) carbonized from (b) different carbonization temperatures and (c) precursors.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Note that the distinctive crystalline structures are observed in XRD patterns, where the peaks at around 24° and 43° give direct information of crystallographic planes of (002) and (100) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). First, the interlayer distances (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) of HCM samples are 4.05 Å (HCM-1000), 3.96 Å (HCM-1200), 3.95 Å (HCM-1400), and 3.80 Å (HCM-1600), respectively. These values are much higher than 3.35 Å of graphite and 3.83 Å of carbon carbonized from PF or sucrose (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). A larger distance is favorable for the (de)intercalation of metallic ions (e.g., Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>). Besides, the microcrystalline structure of HCM changed from disorder to short-range order at an elevated temperature of 1600 °C, as confirmed by the narrower (002) peak in XRD. The results were further confirmed by the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>, attributed to sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms with defects; <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, arises from planar sp<sup>2</sup>-configured carbon atoms) in Raman spectroscopy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The values drop from 3.03 to 1.55 with increasing the temperature from 1000 to 1600 °C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), fully demonstrating the reduction of defects along the graphene sheets. The variation of the HCM microstructure is further confirmed by the HRTEM and SAED (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c–f). The feature of HCMs, mainly consisting of the distinct turbostratic graphitic microstructures without obvious long-range order in structure, is clearly observed as described in a card model. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46, 47)</a> The increased graphitic microcrystallites resulting from the carbon atom rearrangement and the reduction of defects are demonstrated in HRTEM, particularly for the sample of HCM-1600, which is consistent with the results in XRD and Raman spectroscopy. The mainly disordered microstructure of HCMs is further confirmed by SAED patterns, where the sharpness of the diffraction ring increases slightly but no crystal diffraction spots appear even to 1600 °C, which are distinctive features of hard carbon materials.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Comparative XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of hard carbon materials carbonized from the sucrose/PF precursors at different temperatures of 1000 °C (HCM-1000), 1200 °C (HCM-1200), 1400 °C (HCM-1400), and 1600 °C (HCM-1600). Typical TEM and SAED images of (c) HCM-1000, (d) HCM-1200, (e) HCM-1400, and (f) HCM-1600.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Formation Mechanism</h3><div class="NLM_p">To understand the extremely low specific surface of hard carbon and higher yield, the formation mechanism in the presented coupled carbonization strategy is investigated (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). The pyrolysis and carbonization process of precursors in step-sized TGA demonstrates several interesting phenomena to interpret the results. (i) The first, an obvious weight loss of 15 wt %, was observed in the mixture of sucrose/PF at 200 °C, which is much higher than 10 wt % of sucrose and 2 wt % of PF, demonstrating a high degree of intra/intermolecular dehydration and cross-linked polymerization. The initial dehydration (i.e., loss of H<sub>2</sub>O) at a low temperature is positive to avoid the consumption of C by the active H and O atoms at the elevated carbonization temperature, thereby allowing a higher carbon yield of 35%. (ii) Two endothermic peaks at around 240 and 385 °C exist in the derivative of TGA for the carbonization of PF/sucrose mixture, which are different from that of individual sucrose and PF at 295 and 490 °C, respectively. It demonstrates that the carbonization route of sucrose/PF is different starting from the initial dehydration. The much smaller peak area than that of sucrose and the similar carbonization trend as that of PF confirm that the pyrolysis of intermediates and self-consumption of carbon is largely reduced in the presence of PF. Most atoms of H and O in the cross-linked intermediates were released as H<sub>2</sub>O at a relatively low temperature, and then the typical foaming process is limited, facilitating the formation of carbon materials with extremely low surface area (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Step-sized TGA and (b) FTIR spectra of PF, sucrose, and the mixture of sucrose/PF with a mass ratio of 2/1. (c–f) Proposed formation mechanism of hard carbon materials for sucrose/PF precursors toward extremely low surface area.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The interaction of PF and sucrose responsible for the final specific hard carbon was further verified by FTIR. The disappearance of the stretching vibration of free hydroxyl groups (υ<sub>OH</sub>, 3700 and 3551 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and the weakened absorption bands around 1000–1460 cm<sup>–1</sup> (i.e., the C–O–C stretching vibrations and/or O–H bending vibrations of C–OH around at 1079 cm<sup>–1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> fully demonstrate that a strong hydrogen bond exists, and we hypothesize that the PF preferably encapsulates the sucrose inside. Besides, the lower shift of the aromatic skeletal vibrations of the mixture (1600 and 1450 cm<sup>–1</sup>), which may be attributed to the extending of the conjugated unsaturated system and caused by the introduction of p-π conjugated structure between aromatic C═C bonds of PF and oxygen atom from sucrose, further demonstrates the strong interactions of PF and sucrose. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The TGA and FTIR observations can give an interpretation for the results as below. The carbon carbonized from the polymerized PF has a trend to cover the surface of carbon materials mainly obtained from the sucrose. The outside PF can react with sucrose first to release most H and O atoms and cross-link together; in this way it can suppress the foaming process of sucrose, thereby giving rise to an extremely low surface area (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). Also, the cross-linked and branched structure of the combined precursor is preferable to form a large interlayer distance, as confirmed in comparison with the individual precursors (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2 and S5</a>).</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Electrochemical Performances</h3><div class="NLM_p">The typical galvanostatic (dis-)charge curves of HCMs in sodium battery (vs sodium metal) at the rate of 0.1 C are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. So far, there are different opinions regarding the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 42, 50 and 51">(42, 50, 51)</a> Combining our experimental results, it may be concluded that two distinct potential regions exist: (i) the sloping region from 2.0 to 0.15 V, corresponding to the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> in the defects of edges, surface, and porosities of carbon materials and (ii) the plateau region below 0.1 V, mainly attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the graphitic sheets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The capacity contribution from the plateau region increased from 35%, 61%, 69%, to 70% with increasing the carbonization temperature from 1000 °C, 1200 °C, 1400 °C, to 1600 °C, because of the decreased defects and the increased short-range orderly graphitic layers, which are good for hosting the Na<sup>+</sup>. The electrochemical performances are consistent with the structural information in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. Note that most as-prepared carbon materials have a prominent feature of high ICE (&gt;80%) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref30 ref33 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref30 ref33 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 28, 30, 33, 37 to 43, 52 and 53">(28, 30, 33, 37-43, 52, 53)</a> demonstrating the advantages of coupled carbonization strategy preparing low surface area carbon, which can significantly reduce the interfacial reactions between electrode and electrolyte in the formation of SEI. Among these samples, HCM-1400 delivers the highest reversible capacity of 319 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and an impressive ICE of 87%, which is much higher than those directly carbonized from the PF (74%) and sucrose (70%) and even better than 83% of the monodispersed hard carbon spherule coating with soft carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Typical voltage vs capacity profiles of hard carbon materials in the 1st cycle at 0.1C. (b) Comparative ICE of hard carbon materials from different precursors at the current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate capabilities of hard carbon materials from 0.1C to 2C and (d) their cycle performance at 0.1C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The rate capabilities of hard carbon materials are further investigated (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), where the HCM-1400 has the best performance. A high capacity of 296, 285,239, 158, and 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained at the rate of 0.1C, 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, and 2C, respectively; meanwhile, it can recover to 274 and 288 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2C and 0.1C, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The performances are significantly improved compared to those carbons obtained from the individual precursor of PF and sucrose (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), fully demonstrating the advantages of coupled carbonization strategy. The rate capability is deteriorated with increasing carbonization temperature to 1600 °C (HCM-1600), where the lowest capacity of 75 and 54 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was delivered at the high rate of 1C and 2C. The low capacity of HCM-1600 at high rates is mainly related to the rapid capacity fading at the plateau region because of the limited Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion from the decreased defect sites and interlayer distance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). This is consistent with previous reports that defect sites are more favorable for the fast transportation of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby giving rise to a high capacity retention at high rate compared to that in graphitic sheets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> However, a low carbonization temperature cannot give rise to a high capacity, and even a good rate capability can be preserved because of the low electronic conductivity resulting from the insufficient carbonization, such as HCM-1000. Thus, the rate capability mainly depends on the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion constant and the electronic conductivity, and our results confirm that 1400 °C is competent for preparing a high performance hard carbon. Promisingly, all HCMs have a fine cycle performance; in particular, the HCM-1400 retains a high capacity of 287 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 150 cycles, and the capacity retention is as high as 90% (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). Furthermore, the CE can keep around 100% after the initial several cycles, demonstrating the positive effect of low surface area to form a stable SEI layer. Eventually, the good performance should be ascribed to the structural stability of as-prepared carbon materials with large interlayer distance and its unique cross-linked network from combined precursors in comparison with the individual one (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b,c).</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">To understand the (de)sodiation reactions as the (dis-)charge process, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) is carried out to evaluate their kinetic properties. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The apparent diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> in HCM electrodes are measured with a pulse current at 0.1C for 0.5 h with intervals of 2 h. The diffusivity coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) can be estimated based on Fick’s second law with the simplified equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="ueq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/am-2017-05687n_m001.gif" alt=""></span>where τ is the current pulse time (s); <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the active mass in sample (g); <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> are the molar volume and molar mass of B (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>, g mol<sup>–1</sup>); and <i>S</i> is the active surface area of the HCM electrodes (cm<sup>2</sup>). Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> can be obtained from the GITT curves (see details in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S10).</div><div class="NLM_p">The apparent diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> at the plateau section around 0–0.1 V is relatively low, while the diffusion in the sloping region (∼0.15–2.0 V) is faster, demonstrating the much easier sodiation or desodiation in defects than that within the crystalline layered structure. Generally, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity coefficient is in the order of 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, and several important details can be further summarized: (i) A faster decrease of voltage within the sloping region for the HCM-1600 sample in sodiation (i.e., discharge process) demonstrates that a high temperature indeed reduces the defect of hard carbon. (ii) The initial charge voltage of HCM-1000 increased fast (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). It confirms that most Na<sup>+</sup> is stored in the carbon defect rather than within the disordered crystalline layered space, and then it is easy to desodiate. Thus, a good rate capacity can be observed because due to the faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion even the capacity is limited (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). (iii) The HCM-1600 sample demonstrates the most difficulty to achieve a high voltage in desodiation (i.e., charge process). It illustrates that the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalated into the graphitic sheets is difficult to (de)sodiate and has the lowest apparent diffusion coefficient, thereby leading to an inferior rate performance. Thus, two Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms with different Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion constants exist in the hard carbon materials, in which the capacity resulting from the slope region can be well preserved even at the high rate, while that in the plateau region is largely affected by the rate because of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path. The observed results are all consistent with the structural characterizations, and thus the performance can be tuned by the carbonization temperatures.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Comparative voltage vs capacity profiles of hard carbon materials using GITT in the (a) discharge and (b) charge process and their calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the (c) sodiation and (d) desodiation process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Full Battery Applications</h3><div class="NLM_p">To verify the practical applications of as-prepared hard carbon materials, the HCM-1400 is utilized as the anode to match the Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode for a full battery (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The voltage vs capacity profiles of Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>//HCM-1400 full cell at 0.1C (i.e., 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) in the voltage window of 1.0–4.05 V are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. The full cell delivers a high reversible capacity of 289 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (vs carbon anode) after several activation cycles, and a high voltage of 3.2 V with an ICE of 78% is demonstrated. The voltage is comparable and even higher than most Na-ion full batteries in previous literatures, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 55 to 58">(55-58)</a> where most oxides were used as anodes as those in the Li-ion battery, but they still have a safety issue in practical applications due to the volume expansion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55, 56)</a> The studies focused on the utilization of hard carbon as anode in Na-ion full battery are good for its development in commercialization. Even thouogh there is commonly a little bit of low voltage of the Na-ion battery compared with the Li-ion battery, the energy density of this cost-effective battery can achieve as high as 256 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (vs anode), which is much higher than lead-acid batteries and nickel–metal hydride batteries even considering the battery package and irreversible capacity. Besides, the full cell exhibits stable cycle performance with the capacity retention of 85% after 100 cycles and a high capacity of 216 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 160 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even at the high current rate of 1C and 2C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c,d). The high performance and particularly the high capacity retention may benefit from the high CE, and the results suggest that as-prepared HCMs have promising applications in energy storage for Na-ion batteries.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05687n_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Schematic of Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>/HCM-1400 full battery and (b) typical voltage vs capacity profiles in the 1st, 5th, 10th, and 100th cycles at the rate of 0.1C. (c) Cycle performance of the full cell at the rate of 0.2C and (d) its rate curves at various rates of 0.1–2C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/asset/images/large/am-2017-05687n_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05687&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">An efficient coupled carbonization strategy is developed to synthesize hard carbon materials with extremely low surface area and a high carbon yield. A detailed formation mechanism is further presented to understand the uniqueness of the process, and an optimal synthetic condition for the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> in sodium batteries is discussed. The as-prepared hard carbon materials demonstrate great advantages of high initial Coulombic efficiency of 87%, reversible capacity of 319 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and stable capacity retention of 90% beyond 150 cycles. The structural effect of hard carbon materials is further characterized by the GITT, where the difference of Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficient in slope and plateau region is demonstrated. It largely facilitates the tune of Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability according to the carbon structure, which can be finely controlled under the desired carbonization temperature. Besides, the hard carbon materials can match the O3–Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode stably as a full battery, demonstrating a high energy density of 256 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (vs anode) and rate capacities (e.g., 160 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2C). This work not only offers a new coupled strategy to prepare carbon-based materials for versatile applications but also presents fundamental knowledge to understand the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior in hard carbon materials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b05687" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b05687</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Schematic steps for the synthesis and application of hard carbon materials (HCM)s from the mixed precursors of sucrose/phenol formaldehyde resin. SEM, XRD, Raman spectrum, TEM, comparative electrochemical performances of hard carbon materials carbonized from different temperature, and precursors. Varied capacity contrition and cycled voltage vs capacity profiles of different hard carbon materials. Typical one-step in the discharge process for 0.5 h and keep the reaction balance for 2 h before the next step of discharge in the GITT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05687/suppl_file/am7b05687_si_001.pdf">am7b05687_si_001.pdf (894.68 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b05687" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzC8sPf9d5Fg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1126/science.270.5236.590"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mechanisms for lithium insertion in carbonaceous materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dahn, J. R.; Zheng, Tao; Liu, Yinghu; Xue, J. S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, D. C.)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1995</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">270</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5236</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">590-3</span>CODEN:
