        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhO0g3JAit3RA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1126/science.270.5236.590"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review with 24 refs. of mechanisms for lithium insertion in carbonaceous materials.  Lithium can be inserted reversibly within most carbonaceous materials.  The phys. mechanism for this insertion depends on the carbon type.  Lithium intercalates in layered carbons, e.g., graphite, and it adsorbs on the surfaces of single carbon layers in nongraphitizable hard carbons.  Lithium also appears to reversibly bind near hydrogen atoms in carbonaceous materials contg. substantial hydrogen, which are made by heating org. precursors to temps. near 700°.  Each of these three classes of materials is suitable for use as anode in advanced lithium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQcjniVCZFHLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhO0g3JAit3RA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2MXovFGku7g%253D&amp;md5=c3d363fc7fd11848c21d69a70169d145</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.6b01626"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ming, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kumar, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, L.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multilayer Approach for Advanced Hybrid Lithium Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ming, Jun; Li, Mengliu; Kumar, Pushpendra; Li, Lain-Jong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Conventional intercalated rechargeable batteries have shown their capacity limit, and the development of an alternative battery system with higher capacity is strongly needed for sustainable elec. vehicles and hand-held devices.  Herein, a feasible and scalable multilayer approach is introduced to fabricate a promising hybrid lithium battery with superior capacity and multivoltage plateaus.  A sulfur-rich electrode (90 wt. % S) is covered by a dual layer of graphite/Li4Ti5O12, where the active materials S and Li4Ti5O12 can both take part in redox reactions and thus deliver a high capacity of 572 mAh gcathode-1 (vs. the total mass of electrode) or 1866 mAh gs-1 (vs. the mass of sulfur) at 0.1C (with the definition of 1C = 1675 mA gs-1).  The battery shows unique voltage platforms at 2.35 and 2.1 V, contributed from S, and 1.55 V from Li4Ti5O12.  A high rate capability of 566 mAh gcathode-1 at 0.25C and 376 mAh gcathode-1 at 1C with durable cycle ability over 100 cycles can be achieved.  Operando Raman and electron microscope anal. confirm that the graphite/Li4Ti5O12 layer slows the dissoln./migration of polysulfides, thereby giving rise to a higher sulfur utilization and a slower capacity decay.  This advanced hybrid battery with a multilayer concept for marrying different voltage plateaus from various electrode materials opens a way of providing tunable capacity and multiple voltage platforms for energy device applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJPP-4A48BY7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhiKJ5gBy4fAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XpsFGks7c%253D&amp;md5=6d31293cc50be68817df844be5d99c1d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00274"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ming, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kumar, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, A.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wahyudi, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Seeking high-capacity cathodes has become an intensive effort in lithium ion battery research; however, the low energy d. still remains a major issue for sustainable handheld devices and vehicles.  Herein, the authors present a new strategy of integrating a redox species-based electrolyte in batteries to boost their performance.  Taking the olivine LiFePO4-based battery as an example, the incorporation of redox species (i.e., polysulfide of Li2S8) in the electrolyte results in much lower polarization and superior stability, where the dissocd. Li+/Sx2- can significantly speed up the lithium diffusion.  More importantly, the presence of the S82-/S2- redox reaction further contributes extra capacity, making a completely new LiFePO4/Li2Sx hybrid battery with a high energy d. of 1124 Wh kgcathode-1 and a capacity of 442 mAh gcathode-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Although being the std. anode material in Li-ion batteries (LIBs), graphite so far is considered to fail application in Na-ion batteries (NIBs) because the Na-C system lacks suitable binary intercalation compds.  This limitation can be circumvented by using co-intercalation phenomena in a diglyme-based electrolyte.  The resulting compd. is a stage-I ternary intercalation compd. with an estd. stoichiometry of Na(diglyme)2C20.  Highlights of the electrode reaction are its high energy efficiency, the small irreversible loss during the 1st cycle, and a superior cycle life with capacities close to 100 mA-h g-1 for 1000 cycles and coulomb efficiencies &gt;99.87%.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhiKJ5gBy4fAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXksVeqs7g%253D&amp;md5=e4bc2edac8d22121e7986e165b7df007</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murata, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishikawa, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakayama, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ogata, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gotoh, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fujiwara, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention for scalable elec. energy storage considering the abundance and wide availability of Na resources.  However, it remains elusive whether carbon anodes can achieve the similar scale of successes in Na-ion batteries as in Li-ion batteries.  Currently, much attention is focused on hard carbon while soft carbon is generally considered a poor choice.  In this study, we discover that soft carbon can be a high-rate anode in NIBs if the prepn. conditions are carefully chosen.  Furthermore, we discover that the turbostratic lattice of soft carbon is electrochem. expandable, where d-spacing rises from 3.6 to 4.2 Å.  Such a scale of lattice expansion only due to the Na-ion insertion was not known for carbon materials.  It is further learned that portions of such lattice expansion are highly reversible, resulting in excellent cycling performance.  Moreover, soft carbon delivers a good capacity at potentials above 0.2 V, which enables an intrinsically dendrite-free anode for NIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2ebw_xci75bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8Jah1fjOIug"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitVanur%252FO&amp;md5=5f5a09bcf197b9076f295038bf5d2abd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00172"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Surta, T.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The capacity of hard carbon anodes in Na-ion batteries rarely reaches values beyond 300 mAh/g.  It is reported that doping POx into local structures of hard carbon increases its reversible capacity from 283-359 mAh/g.  It is confirmed that the doped POx is redox inactive by X-ray adsorption near edge structure measurements, thus not contributing to the higher capacity.  Two significant changes are obsd. of hard carbon's local structures caused by doping.  First, the (002) d-spacing inside the turbostratic nanodomains is increased, revealed by both lab. and synchrotron X-ray diffraction.  Second, doping turns turbostratic nanodomains more defective along ab planes, indicated by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.  The local structural changes of hard carbon are correlated to the higher capacity, where both the plateau and slope regions in the potential profiles are enhanced.  The study demonstrates that Na-ion storage in hard carbon heavily depends on carbon local structures, where such structures, despite being disordered, can be tuned toward unusually high capacities.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqdu855Pkblf7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8Jah1fjOIug"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFOktLvL&amp;md5=1451c96d6ecdb7b2271ab49cd240507b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1039/c2cc17129e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kovarik, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nie, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity, Reversible Alloying Reactions in Snsb/C Nanocomposites for Na-Ion Battery Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are a potential substitute to Li-ion batteries for energy storage devices.  However, their poor electrochem. performance, esp. capacity and rate capability, is the major bottleneck to future development.  Here we propose a performance-oriented electrode structure, which is 1D nanostructure arrays with large-scale high ordering, good vertical alignment, and large interval spacing.  Benefiting from these structural merits, a great enhancement in electrochem. performance could be achieved.  Taking Sb as an example, we firstly report large-scale highly ordered Sb nanorod arrays with uniform large interval spacing (190 nm).  In return for this electrode design, high ion accessibility, fast electron transport, and strong electrode integrity are presented here.  Used as additive- and binder-free anodes for Na-ion batteries, Sb nanorod arrays showed a high capacity of 620 mA h g-1 at the 100th cycle with a retention of 84% up to 250 cycles at 0.2 A g-1, and a superior rate capability for delivering reversible capacities of 579.7 and 557.7 mA h g-1 at 10 and 20 A g-1, resp.  A full cell coupled by a P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 cathode and a Sb nanorod array anode was also constructed, which showed good cycle performance up to 250 cycles, high rate capability up to 20 A g-1, and large energy d. up to 130 Wh kg-1.  These excellent electrochem. performances shall pave the way for developing more applications of Sb nanorod arrays in energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpRS7y0VfakjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8Jah1fjOIug"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtFGns7nO&amp;md5=50b74924c2a93cc137e8a9993ce641fa</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1021/jz2012066"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiong, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are an attractive alternative to lithium-ion batteries that alleviate problems with lithium availability and cost.  Despite several studies of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries involving layered oxide materials, there are few low-voltage metal oxide anodes capable of operating sodium ion reversibly at room temp.  We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Black phosphorous (BP) is a unique layered p-type semiconducting material.  The successful use of BP nanosheets in field-effect transistors fueled research on BP at. layers that focuses on, e.g., the exploration of their optical and electronic properties, and promising applications in (opto)electronics.  However, BP films are prone to degrdn. in ambient conditions, which prevents their com. application.  Here, a route to the application of BP films as an environmental stable nonvolatile resistive random access memory is presented.  The BP films, which are prepd. from exfoliated BP nanosheets in selected solvents, show solvent-dependent degrdn. upon ambient exposure, inducing the formation of an metallic glasses top degraded layer (TDL).  The TDL acts as an insulating barrier just below the Al electrode.  This property that was only obtained by degrdn., confers a bipolar resistive switching behavior with a high ON/OFF current ratio up to ∼3 × 105 and excellent retention ability over 105 s to the flexible BP memory devices.  The TDL also prevents propagation of degrdn. further into the film, ensuring excellent memory performance even after three month of ambient exposure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrwKXZOM7SOLLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhMgKo5YRPQ3g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xit1Sju78%253D&amp;md5=82eade455c671cd7b257d6241385b763</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b02833"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Manthiram, A.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Bi<sub>0.94</sub>Sb<sub>1.06</sub>S<sub>3</sub> Nanorod Cluster Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Enhanced Reversibility by the Synergistic Effect of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>-Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> Solid Solution</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. The Bi0.94Sb1.06S3-graphite composite obtained by a mech. milling of 80% Bi0.94Sb1.06S3 and 20% graphite exhibits much improved cycle stability with 79% capacity retention after 200 cycles in the full voltage window of 0.01-2.8 V compared to its Bi2S3-graphite (58% retention) and Sb2S3-graphite (10% retention) counterparts, demonstrating a synergistic effect.  Cyclic voltammetry scans indicate that the polarization overpotential assocd. with the conversion reaction in Bi0.94Sb1.06S3 is lower than those in Sb2S3 and Bi2S3.  Cycling under controlled voltage windows of 2.8-0.85 V corresponding to a conversion reaction and 0.85-0.01 V corresponding to an alloying reaction reveals both highly reversible conversion and alloying reactions in the Bi0.94Sb1.06S3 solid soln. anode compared to that in the binary counterpart anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGofJeulevL4x7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhMgKo5YRPQ3g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlajtbzL&amp;md5=c60500c971629c8d5a41de520e617422</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee22864e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abouimrane, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Weng, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Eltayeb, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Niklas, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Poluektov, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Amine, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Insertion in Carboxylate Based Materials and Their Application in 3.6 V Full Sodium Cells</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The sodium battery has the potential to be the next generation rechargeable system which utilizes cheaper and more abundant sodium material but affords nearly the same power as lithium batteries.  One of the key barriers for the sodium battery is the lack of stable anode materials which can insert sodium ions reversibly at relatively low potential.  This contribution reports the sodium insertion in a series of org. carboxylate based materials: (C8H4Na2O4), (C8H6O4), (C8H5NaO4), (C8Na2F4O4), (C10H2Na4O8), (C14H4O6) and (C14H4Na4O8) at low voltage (below 0.6 V vs. Na/Na+).  These org. anode materials can insert reversibly up to 2 Na per mol. with good cycleability.  The Na insertion mechanism was proposed and 3.6 V full sodium batteries were made and cycled reversibly at room temp. and at 55 °C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqohlWV-41Oy7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh9lv00JWeTMA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFCjs7bE&amp;md5=9c98061a338ffda869b2748427bc4a2a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201400083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hasa, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Buchholz, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Passerini, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Scrosati, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hassoun, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Performance Na<sub>0.5</sub>[Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.13</sub>Mn<sub>0.63</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries. Despite this, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, the authors synthesized monodispersed hard carbon spherules (HCS) from an abundant biomass of sucrose, and studied the influence of the carbonization temp. on the microstructure and electrochem. performance.  The initial coulombic efficiency of the HCS was increased to 83% by coating its surface with soft carbon through the pyrolysis of toluene.  The plateau capacity at the low potential region increased with increasing carbonization temp.  The HCS carbonized at 1600° showed the highest plateau capacity (220 mA-h/g) and excellent cycling performance with a capacity retention of 93% after 100 cycles.  When coupled with an air-stable P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 pos. electrode, the full cell exhibited a high initial coulombic efficiency of 76%, a mean operating voltage of 3.5 V and excellent cycling performance.  The theor. energy d. of this system is 200 W-h/kg.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are emerging as one of the most promising energy storage technologies, particularly for grid-level applications.  Among anode candidate materials, hard carbon is very attractive due to its high capacity and low cost.  However, hard carbon anodes often suffer a low first-cycle coulombic efficiency and fast capacity fading.  In this study, we discover that doping graphene oxide into sucrose, the precursor for hard carbon, can effectively reduce the sp. surface area of hard carbon to as low as 5.4 m2/g.  We further show that such doping can effectively prevent foaming during caramelization of sucrose and extend the pyrolysis burnoff of sucrose caramel over a wider temp. range.  The obtained low-surface-area hard carbon greatly improves the first-cycle coulombic efficiency from 74% to 83% and delivers a very stable cycle life with 95% of capacity retention after 200 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrqaHV_SLIfobVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh9lv00JWeTMA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXks1KlsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=4b387ca5b76e59789c78130fa5560be4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1039/c3gc40480c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ming, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple surfactant-assisted reflux method was used in this study for the synthesis of cocklebur-shaped Fe2O3 nanoparticles (NPs).  With this strategy, nanostructured Fe2O3 NPs with a size distribution ranging from 20 to 120 nm and a tunable surface area were readily controlled by varying reflux temp. and the type of surfactant.  Surfactants such as cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB), polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP), poly(ethylene glycol)-block-poly(propylene glycol)-block-poly(ethylene glycol) (F127) and Na dodecyl benzenesulfonate (SDBS) were used to achieve large-scale synthesis of uniform Fe2O3 NPs with a relatively low cost.  A new composite of Fe3O4@CFx was prepd. by coating the primary Fe2O3 NPs with a layer of F-doped C (CFx) with a 1-step carbonization process.  The Fe3O4@CFx composite was used as the anode in a Li ion battery and exhibited a high reversible capacity of 900 mA-h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 over 100 cycles with 95% capacity retention.  A new Fe3O4@CFx/LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 battery with a high energy d. of 371 Wh kg-1 (vs. cathode) was successfully assembled, and &gt;300 cycles were easily completed with 66.8% capacity retention at 100 mA g-1.  Even cycled at the high temp. of 45°, this full cell also exhibited a relatively high capacity of 91.6 mA-h g-1 (vs. cathode) at 100 mA g-1 and retained 54.6% of its reversible capacity over 50 cycles.  Introducing CFx chems. to modify metal oxide anodes and/or any other cathode is of great interest for advanced energy storage and conversion devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoGWaTWLbFZerVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgnTi-qJZgpdQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtl2rtLvO&amp;md5=501b27a79e4fc8d606fff5c498a64410</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA08601A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a promising candidate for grid electricity storage due to their potential low cost.  The development of anode materials is a crucial step to promote the commercialization of SIBs, and amorphous carbon materials are likely to be the most promising alternatives among all proposed anode materials.  However, the cost of the reported carbon materials is still very high due to the expensive precursors and their low carbon yield.  Here, we report an amorphous carbon (AC) material made from low cost pitch.  The amorphous carbon material with an amazing high carbon yield of 57% was achieved by utilizing the emulsification interaction between pitch and lignin to suppress the graphitization of pitch during the carbonization.  The effects of heat-treatment temps. and the pitch/lignin mass ratios on the morphol., microstructure and the electrochem. performance of AC were systematically investigated.  By optimizing exptl. conditions, we achieved one representative AC with a suitable morphol. and microstructure, which exhibits promising performances with a high reversible capacity of 254 mA h g-1, a high initial coulombic efficiency of 82% and excellent cycling stability.  This is the first demonstration that the pitch can be successfully applied in fabricating amorphous carbon anode materials for SIBs with superior low cost and high performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzLQKBB7Gqa7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGI8JMIQcrMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslyksbnK&amp;md5=a8c8540d06fbad13618a164da2e6d5c5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA04877C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The utilization of renewable energies has become increasingly urgent for the sustainable development of our society.  Energy storage systems are essential in order to efficiently use these energies.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) show bright prospect in the application for energy storage markets due to the potential low cost originating from unlimited sources and wide distribution of Na.  However, the anode remains a great challenge in the industrialization of SIBs.  Hard carbon holds the most promising future among all reported anodes; however, there are still two main shortcomings such as high cost and low initial coulombic efficiency, which limit its application.  Here, we report a hard carbon material derived from an abundant and abandoned biomass of corn cobs (HCC) using a simple carbonization method.  The HCC shows excellent sodium storage performance with a reversible capacity of ca. 300 mA h g-1, a high initial coulombic efficiency of 86% and good cycling stability.  A prototype sodium-ion battery was prepd. to prove the application prospect using HCC1300 as the anode and Na0.9[Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.48]O2 as the cathode, exhibiting a high energy d. of 207 W h kg-1 and a long cycle life.  These excellent properties demonstrate that HCC is a potential candidate as an anode material for sodium-ion battery application.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzP3444VBrIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGI8JMIQcrMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht1eju7nN&amp;md5=f6776d35bde5f3fb873f88fe495141b6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref39" class="rightTabRefNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit39" data-doi="10.1016/j.jechem.2016.04.016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Simone, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Boulineau, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">de Geyer, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rouchon, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Simonin, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Martinet, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon Derived from Cellulose as Anode for Sodium Ion Batteries: Dependence of Electrochemical Properties on Structure</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon materials have attracted great interest as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high performance and low cost.  Here, natural wood fiber derived hard carbon anodes are studied for SIBs considering the abundance and low cost of wood.  It is discovered that a thermal carbonization of wood fiber led to a porous carbon with a high sp. surface area of 586 m2 g-1, while a pretreatment with 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) could effectively decrease it to 126 m2 g-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqegbEbsDPzrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGI8JMIQcrMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1Oqsb3M&amp;md5=a2144d9fa8c9f19a479ebec0ec74f1eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600659"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple and industrial scalable approach was developed to encapsulate metal oxide nanocrystals into porous carbon (PC) with a high distribution.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This work presents a systematic study of the ratio between the integrated intensities of the disorder-induced D and G Raman bands (ID/IG) in nanographite samples with different crystallite sizes (La) and using different excitation laser energies.                         rightTab-cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical insertion of sodium into hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Thomas, P.; Billaud, D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. insertion of sodium ions into different types of hard carbons was achieved in electrolytes composed of ethylene carbonate as the solvent and NaClO4 as the salt.  For all the materials studied the sodium uptake increases when the carbon highest heat treatment temp. (HTT) decreases.  PAN-based carbon fibers appear to be suitable structures to allow significant sodium insertion.  Thus, T650 ex-PAN fibers lead to a reversible capacity close to 209 mAh g-1.  In that case, sodium insertion occurs in two main ways: one is the adsorption on the single graphene layers and the other is the concomitant insertion into the porosity that occurs below 0.1 V vs. Na+/Na.  This second mechanism, which is indicated by a low-voltage plateau on the electrochem. curves, allows significant insertion.  The compared electrochem. study of two saccharose-coke samples corresponding to different regions of Dahn's classification underlines the importance of the carbon precursor and of the manuf. process.  The reversible capacity is equal to 184 mAh g-1 for the sample heat treated at 800 °C which presents a high hydrogen content whereas it is close to 145 mAh g-1 for the one characterized by a HTT of about 1500 °C and a low hydrogen content.  The best electrochem. performances are obtained for pyrolyzed cellulose carbons.  Indeed, the reversible capacity is about 279 mAh g-1.  Outgassing these carbons at 950°C results in such a decrease of the reversible capacity down to 145 mAh g-1.  That can be related either to the thermal elimination of heteroelements or to modifications of the pore size distribution.  Consequently, the most suitable hard carbon material for anodic applications in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries should both present a high residual hydrogen content and a significant microporosity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpJDGOzzH5pW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDnqvo97dZQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38Xlslylsrg%253D&amp;md5=54dfbedb15fd432f5c3abc03b64cd1a2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-area phosphorus-doped carbon nanosheets (P-CNSs) are first obtained from carbon dots (CDs) through self-assembly driving from thermal treatment with Na catalysis.  This is the first time to realize the conversion from 0D CDs to 2D nanosheets doped with phosphorus.  The sodium storage behavior of phosphorus-doped carbon material is also investigated for the first time.  As anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), P-CNSs exhibit superb performances for electrochemical storage of sodium.                         rightTab-cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoporous hard carbon anodes for improved electrochemical performance in sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Prabakar, S. J. Richard; Jeong, Jaehyang; Pyo, Myoungho</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The porosity and morphol. of sucrose-based hard carbon (SHC) was regulated by varying the amt. of bicarbonate salts added during a simple two-stage sintering process.  During the first-stage thermal treatment of sugar at 200°, CO2 liberated from bicarbonate contributed to the pulverization of particles and to the formation of submicron-sized pores.  Na2CO3 entrapped in a precursor matrix also released CO2 during the second-stage sintering at 850°, producing nanometric pores (∼10 nm in diam.).  The excessively high content of bicarbonates, however, resulted in paper-thin graphitic layers with no submicron-sized pores.  These dual roles of bicarbonates produced nanoporous SHC (NSHC) with the submicron-to-nano-sized pores and the largest surface area that was possible for a specific bicarbonate concn.  The optimal nanoporosity of NSHC lent itself to a sharp increase in reversible capacity.  Reversible capacity of 324 and 289 mA h g-1 were obtained for the first and 100th cycles at 20 mA g-1, in contrast to 251 and 213 mA h g-1, resp., for SHC.                         rightTab-cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards high energy density sodium ion batteries through electrolyte optimization</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ponrouch, Alexandre; Dedryvere, Remi; Monti, Damien; Demet, Atif E.; Ateba Mba, Jean Marcel; Croguennec, Laurence; Masquelier, Christian; Johansson, Patrik; Palacin, M. Rosa</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2361-2369</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is reported entailing optimization of sodium ion electrolyte formulation and compatibility studies with pos. and neg. electrode materials.  EC:PC:DMC and EC:PC:DME were found to exhibit optimum ionic conductivities and lower viscosities.  Yet, hard carbon neg. electrode materials tested in such electrolytes exhibit significant differences in performance, rooted in the different resistivity of the SEI, which results in too large polarization and concomitant loss of capacity at low potentials when DME is used as a co-solvent.  EC0.45:PC0.45:DMC0.1 was found to be the optimum compn. resulting in good rate capability and high capacity upon sustained cycling for hard carbon electrodes.  Its compatibility with pos. Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF) electrodes was also confirmed, which led to the assembly of full Na-ion cells displaying an operation voltage of 3.65 V, very low polarization and excellent capacity retention upon cycling with ca. 97 mA h g-1 of NVPF after more than 120 cycles together with satisfactory coulombic efficiency (&gt;98.5%) and very good power performance.  Such values lead to energy densities comparable to those of the current state-of-the-art lithium-ion technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrWWc8cwpdbDbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDnqvo97dZQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisr7M&amp;md5=3b08f3921d6438d33b14eb6e5184904a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1021/jp9113333"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C. S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique for Phase-Transformation Electrodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Phys. Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique for Phase-Transformation Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yujie; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">114</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2830-2841</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT) and a novel potentiostatic intermittent titrn. technique (PITT) for phase-transformation electrodes were developed by integrating mixed control phase-transformation theory with traditional GITT and PITT methods.  The contribution of the strain accommodation energy to the thermodn. driving force for phase transformation was assessed.  These novel GITT and PITT methods can be used to det. the true ion diffusion coeff. and the interface mobility of phase-transformation electrodes in the two-phase region.  To demonstrate the utility of this method, the lithium ion diffusion coeff. and the interface mobility of two LiFePO4 samples with different particle sizes were obtained in the two-phase region.  The lithium ion diffusion coeff. in the two-phase region as measured using phase-transformation GITT was on the order of 10-13 cm2/s in the β phase (Li1-yFePO4) and 10-12 cm2/s in the α phase (LixFeO4), which is similar to the diffusion coeffs. in the single β and single α phase regions detd. using traditional GITT and PITT.  This similarity with the diffusion-coeff.-validated phase-transformation GITT and PITT is expected since traditional GITT/PITT is reliable in the single-phase region.  The interface mobility of the LiFePO4 (∼10-15 mmol/(J·s)) increases with decreasing particle size.  The interface mobility of the LiFePO4/FePO4 during electrochem. discharge at room temp. is comparable to that of the martensite-austenite transformation in an Fe-C alloy with a semicoherence interface at 350°.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpCWR-d0jjNibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDnqvo97dZQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXptlyqtw%253D%253D&amp;md5=1a9617e940b052e795f5036dcda956e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7865"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Delivery of high capacity with good retention is a challenge in developing cathodes for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.  Here we present a radially aligned hierarchical columnar structure in spherical particles with varied chem. compn. from the inner end (Na[Ni0.75Co0.02Mn0.23]O2) to the outer end (Na[Ni0.58Co0.06Mn0.36]O2) of the structure.  With this cathode material, we show that an electrochem. reaction based on Ni2+/3+/4+ is readily available to deliver a discharge capacity of 157 mAh (g-oxide)-1 (15 mA g-1), a capacity retention of 80% (125 mAh g-1) during 300 cycles in combination with a hard carbon anode, and a rate capability of 132.6 mAh g-1 (1,500 mA g-1, 10 C-rate).  The cathode also exhibits good temp. performance even at -20°C.  These results originate from rather unique chem. of the cathode material, which enables the Ni redox reaction and minimizes the surface area contacting corrosive electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFrnMsHRA0bbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDnqvo97dZQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXotlGks7w%253D&amp;md5=82d545d260f675de396361335b9ab7ea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1039/C6EE00794E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Peng, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An Advanced High-Energy Sodium Ion Full Battery Based on Nanostructured Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>/VOPO<sub>4</sub> Layered Materials</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In virtue of the abundant sodium resources, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered to be one of the promising alternatives to lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  However, current research concs. mostly on sodium ion half-cells, and the development of sodium ion full cells with high performance remains a crit. challenge.  Here we rationally designed a full sodium-ion battery based on nanostructured Na2Ti3O7 and VOPO4 materials as the anodes and cathodes, owing to their advantageous electrochem. features.  The full cell outputs one of the highest operating voltages close to 2.9 V and delivers a large reversible capacity of 114 mA h g-1 at a rate of 0.1C.  It also shows outstanding rate capability (∼74 mA h g-1 at 2C rate) and excellent cycling stability (92.4% capacity retention after 100 cycles).  A high energy d. of 220 W h kg-1 is achieved, which is comparable to the state-of-the-art LIBs.  Moreover, the temp.-dependent charge-discharge tests indicate excellent capacity retentions in a wide temp. range of -20 to 55 °C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoeiSHi_Z7ig7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-9fWOBXKN2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVCmsb7J&amp;md5=e38751b094eee46fa6320e8370c3026b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref57" class="rightTabRefNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit57" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA14733B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ming, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ming, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kwak, W.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, J.-B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Y. K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Sustainable Iron-Based Sodium Ion Battery of Porous Carbon-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with High Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">RSC Adv.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">5</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">8793</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">8800</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A sustainable iron-based sodium ion battery of porous carbon-Fe3O4/Na2FeP2O7 with high performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ming, Jun; Ming, Hai; Yang, Wenjing; Kwak, Won-Jin; Park, Jin-Bum; Zheng, Junwei; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A type of porous carbon-Fe3O4 (e.g., PC-Fe3O4) composite with an industrially scalable prodn. was introduced in the sodium ion battery application for the first time.  The PC-Fe3O4 composite, consisting of highly dispersed Fe3O4 nanocrystals within the porous carbon with a relatively low wt. percent of 45.5 wt%, could efficiently demonstrate high capacities of 225, 168, 127, 103, 98 and 90 mA h g-1 under the current densities of 50, 100, 200, 300, 400 and 500 mA g-1 with a good stability over 400 cycles.  The utilization co-efficient of Fe3O4 nanocrystals was proven to be much higher than most of the Fe3O4 nanoparticles reported recently via the study of the capacity contribution of carbon originally.  In addn., the robustness of electrode during the charge-discharge was well characterized by ex situ XRD and emission SEM (SEM).  More importantly, a new concept of an elemental iron-based sodium ion battery of PC-Fe3O4/Na2FeP2O7 is presented.  This is the first example to introduce an element-rich configuration in the sodium ion battery from the viewpoint of sustainability.  The full battery demonstrated a superior capacity of 93 mA h g-1, high capacity retention of 93.3% over 100 cycles and work voltage around 2.28 V with the energy d. of 203 W h kg-1.  Such configuration of an iron-based sodium battery would be highly promising and sustainable owing to its low cost and high stability in grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqlfLDULELEl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-9fWOBXKN2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFehtb7J&amp;md5=dbdb474ad5240a7412c37b8740147f9c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref58" class="rightTabRefNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit58" data-doi="10.1021/nl500077v"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oh, S.                         rightTab-cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Na[Ni0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25]O2/C-Fe3O4 Sodium-Ion Batteries Using EMS Electrolyte for Energy Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Oh, Seung-Min; Myung, Seung-Taek; Yoon, Chong Seung; Lu, Jun; Hassoun, Jusef; Scrosati, Bruno; Amine, Khalil; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">While much research effort was devoted to the development of advanced Li-ion batteries for renewal energy storage applications, the Na-ion battery is also of considerable interest because Na is one of the most abundant elements in the Earth's crust.  The authors report a Na-ion battery based on a C-coated Fe3O4 anode, Na[Ni0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25]O2 layered cathode, and NaClO4 in fluoroethylene carbonate and Et methanesulfonate electrolyte.  This unique battery system combines an intercalation cathode and a conversion anode, resulting in high capacity, high rate capability, thermal stability, and much improved cycle life.         <script nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	});

<script nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e8af69a9775c-SJC">
"use strict"
//Script checks gdpr cookie and submits form
	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point
	*/

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {

)|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});

    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
